# Layout Hell-o

_175 messages · 21 participants · 2004-07 → 2004-08_

---

### Re: Layout Hell-o

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-07-29T17:01:24-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
I wrote:

> [Note that there are three common accurate representations for the value
> (-1) in that description --  ... and @4C8000000000000000000001@, canonic
> double-precision integer, ...

Wrong.  My mistake.  That's -68,719,476,736.  The value for (1) is
@468000000000000000000001@.

For grins, while I'm at it, for those who care, turns out there seems to be
27, not 26, exact representations of integers < 8 in double precision on
Unisys MCP systems.  Using (-7) as an example, they are:
    @667000000000000000000000@, = normalized USAGE DOUBLE
    @658E00000000000000000000@
    @6501C0000000000000000000@
    @648038000000000000000000@
    @640007000000000000000000@
    @638000E00000000000000000@
    @6300001C0000000000000000@
    @628000038000000000000000@
    @620000007000000000000000@
    @618000000E00000000000000@
    @61000000001C000000000000@
    @608000000003800000000000@
    @400000000007000000000000@, extended single-integer
    @600000000007000000000000@ (forgot this one:  ditto, with (-0) in the
exponent).
    @408000000000007000000000@
    @410000000000000E00000000@
    @4180000000000001C0000000@
    @420000000000000038000000@
    @428000000000000007000000@
    @430000000000000000E00000@
    @4380000000000000001C0000@
    @44000000000000000000E000@
    @448000000000000000007000@
    @450000000000000000000380@
    @4580000000000000000000E0@
    @46000000000000000000001C@
    @468000000000000000000007@, canonic double-integer.

The corresponding single-precision representations are given by lopping off
the rightmost 48 bits of zeroes from the first fourteen of these.  Thus:
    @667000000000@, = normalized USAGE REAL
    ...
    @400000000007@, canonic single-integer
    @600000000007@, ditto with (-0) in the exponent field


And, back to the topic of a standard-notation representation of records, any
implementation-independent description of information in these formats had
better be prepared to understand all of them, and why they all represent the
same value, to say nothing of what that value is ...

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
CS> And, back to the topic of a standard-notation representation of
CS> records, any implementation-independent description of information in
CS> these formats had better be prepared to understand all of them, and
CS> why they all represent the same value, to say nothing of what that
CS> value is ...

   I don't think there is any sense in implementation-independent  
descriptions of very implementation-specific representations of data.

   What is necessary, is to have generic formats and their description  
for information INTERCHANGE as the ANSI standards for interchange  
magnetic TAPES, extended to current media for information exchange -  
CD-ROMs, ZIP-Disks, network.

   I think e.g. of the floating point format as specified by IEEE; on  
the other hand, I think that machine-specific formats as the 'little- 
endian' binaries as used internally by Intel-CPUs should have no place  
in files for information interchange.


Yours,
```

```
l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:

>.    Am  29.07.04
>schrieb  charles.stevens@unisys.com (Chuck Stevens)
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>descriptions of very implementation-specific representations of data.

In the last 30 years, I haven't described a file with COMP-? types. COMP is for
internal efficiency; files should be 100% DISPLAY.

>   What is necessary, is to have generic formats and their description  
>for information INTERCHANGE as the ANSI standards for interchange  
>magnetic TAPES, extended to current media for information exchange -  
>CD-ROMs, ZIP-Disks, network.

Forget INTERCHANGE; think XML. Even for 'internal' files. You never know when
management will offer to exchange the files with outsiders, as happened to
Carol.

Companies in Financial Services Data Warehousing -- Reuters, Bloomberg, Thomson
and Morningstar -- are rapidly moving to making XML a standard for internal as
well as interchange. I worked at one of them last year. We exchanged
multi-megabytes daily, and none of it came via tape.

>   I think e.g. of the floating point format as specified by IEEE; on  
>the other hand, I think that machine-specific formats as the 'little- 
>endian' binaries as used internally by Intel-CPUs should have no place  
>in files for information interchange.

They have no place in ANY files.
```

```
.     Am  30.07.04
 schrieb  robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
     bei  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
      in  410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net
   ueber  Re: Layout Hell-o

RW>
RW> In the last 30 years, I haven't described a file with COMP-? types.
RW> COMP is for internal efficiency; files should be 100% DISPLAY.

   That depends ... if the element stored is mostly just presented to  
a human viewer, or if it is used in computations.

   And then, for space efficiency, one would compress the file, and  
uncompress it again for usage -- that may be more computational effort  
than converting a binary number for display. Also think of binary  
storage as a kind of data compression ...

   How do the database tables look like which are being used?

RW> XML a standard for internal as well as interchange.

   I had XML in mind ...

>>               , I think that machine-specific formats as the 'little-
>> endian' binaries as used internally by Intel-CPUs should have no place
>> in files for information interchange.

RW> They have no place in ANY files.

   Well, look at the various UTF-16 representations of Unicode -- I  
still remember how Internet-oriented people were opposed to the  
established multinational character sets like Teletex (ISO 6937 or  
CCITT T.61) or the code switching techniques of ISO 2022, which are  
widely being used in Japan.

   And now look at what comes out of Unicode: three variants of UTF- 
16: UTF-16LE with little-endian representation of the character  
numbers, UTF-16BE with big-endian, and a third format, where the  
'endian-ness' is being described in the first 16 bits of the file.

   You will have that in XML, too.


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.mlwerke.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

"Die Interessen der Nation lassen sich nicht anders formulieren als unter
dem Gesichtspunkt der herrschenden Klasse oder der Klasse, die die
Herrschaft anstrebt."            - Leo Trotzki         (27. Januar 1932)
```

```
l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:

>RW>
>RW> In the last 30 years, I haven't described a file with COMP-? types.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>a human viewer, or if it is used in computations.

If it is used in computations, move it to working-storage. 

>   And then, for space efficiency, one would compress the file, and  
>uncompress it again for usage -- that may be more computational effort  
>than converting a binary number for display. Also think of binary  
>storage as a kind of data compression ...

Ironically, people who claim to conserve disk space are the same ones who pad
the record with 100 bytes 'for future expansion' .. and put arrays in records,
thereby guaranteeing every record is worst-case. Why? Because someone told them
variable-length records are Bad (based on 30 year-old experience) and someone
else told them arrays are better than lists (because he once had troubles with
pointers, and doesn't know about normalized database design).

Application programmers are amateurs out of their depth when it comes to
managing data files. The job is better done by professionals who write
databases.

Some file systems do compress and expand individual records as they are
accessed. It is almost free because they do it in parallel threads. The main
process doesn't have to wait for an output record to be compressed before
proceeding. 

>   How do the database tables look like which are being used?

Do you mean how do they look on disk? Most databases store numbers as
large-precision floating-point .. large enough to avoid rounding errors.

>   And now look at what comes out of Unicode: three variants of UTF- 
>16: UTF-16LE with little-endian representation of the character  
>numbers, UTF-16BE with big-endian, and a third format, where the  
>'endian-ness' is being described in the first 16 bits of the file.

Those are religious wars. Standards are supposed to eliminate, not legitimize
them. 

>   You will have that in XML, too.

Nope. XML does not allow raw binary; it must be encoded MIME/Base64.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> Ironically, people who claim to conserve disk space are the same ones who pad
> the record with 100 bytes 'for future expansion' 

Which specific people did both of these at the same time ?

In fact when compression is used, 'padding' takes no disk space at all
(it just makes a bigger number in the RLL code).

> and someone
> else told them arrays are better than lists (because he once had troubles with
> pointers, and doesn't know about normalized database design).

Someone attempted to tell us here that lists were 'better' than
pointers, but, in fact, it turned out they were slower and less
flexible.

> Application programmers are amateurs out of their depth when it comes to
> managing data files. The job is better done by professionals who write
> databases.

Is that _all_ application programmers ? or just the ones that you have
met ?
 
> Some file systems do compress and expand individual records as they are
> accessed. It is almost free because they do it in parallel threads. The main
> process doesn't have to wait for an output record to be compressed before
> proceeding. 

It may, or may not, use 'separate threads' depending on the actual
system.  The _actual_ advantage of compression (apart from space
saving) is that fewer disk transfers are required, this being usually
a much larger saving than the cost of compressing and decompressing.
 
> Do you mean how do they look on disk? Most databases store numbers as
> large-precision floating-point .. large enough to avoid rounding errors.

Databases also store fixed point numerics as scaled integers.
 
> Nope. XML does not allow raw binary; it must be encoded MIME/Base64.

It allows binary represented as 6bit characters scaled to the base64
set.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

> The _actual_ advantage of compression (apart from space
>saving) is that fewer disk transfers are required, this being usually
>a much larger saving than the cost of compressing and decompressing.

The spread between computer and disk speed will continue to increase. Computers
double in speed every 18 months. Disks advance more slowly, and may already have
hit the physical limit set by strength of materials. 

Processors now use trace width of .1 microns = 100 angstroms. The smallest
possible conductor measures 4 angstroms. That means we have four 18-month
generations, six years, to go before hitting the wall. What then?

>> Nope. XML does not allow raw binary; it must be encoded MIME/Base64.
>
>It allows binary represented as 6bit characters scaled to the base64
>set.

That's intended for non-text objects such as jpgs, not for numbers represented
in binary.
```

- **Date:** 2004-08-02T02:55:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<410d908d.83255874@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <9Dp3u7wuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net> <9DtCFL09flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410c13bd.106316921@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408010014.3b7440dd@posting.google.com> <410cfe7e.45858953@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408011404.11000079@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>> The spread between computer and disk speed will continue to increase.
Computers
>> double in speed every 18 months. Disks advance more slowly, and may already
have
>> hit the physical limit set by strength of materials. 
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>good thing.

That's what I said. My argument was directly related.

>In any case your 'hit the physical limit' is what was being said by
>shortsighted pundits before transistors were invented.

CDs and magneto optical disks are made of polycarbonate, which has a tensile
strength of 60 MPa (million Pascals = million Newtons per square meter). They
spin at up to 20,000 rpm, which is the physical limit. Any faster would make
them shatter. 

Hard disks are made of aluminum, which has a tensile strength of 250 MPa (same
as steel) . They now spin at up to 10K rpm. They cannot go four times faster
than polycarbonate because they're heavier. The theoretical max is around 20K
rpm. 

Kevlar has tensile strength of 1250 MPa and is light. A Kevlar disk could
theoretically go above 100K rpm. Problem is, it's expensive. Bigger problem is,
the outside surface would be traveling at 1900 Km/hr = 1200 m/hr .. about twice
the speed of sound. It would have to be housed in a vacuum to avoid a sonic boom
and would have to be heavily shielded for safety in case of failure.

For comparison, a dentist's drill goes 100K rpm. It doesn't break the sound
barrier because the bit has a small circumference. 

Carbon nanotubes have a tensile strength of 65-200 GPa. In theory, they could
spin at an incredible 10M rpm. The limiting factor would be the strength of
surface coating -- the metal or polymer holding the information. 

Decades ago, non-mechanical storage was thought to be the answer. Remember
Bubble Memory? It turned out to be impractical because bubbles collided at the
figure-eight intersection .. like an electronic demolition derby.

On the solid-state front, Compact Flash, Secure Digital (SD) and SONY offer
inexpensive cards holding 512M. Smart Media stopped at 128M. Why? Because Smart
Media hit the physical wall. Short-sighted companies (in your judgment) who
hitched their wagon to SM have been forced to change direction e.g. Olympus.

I doubt solid-state storage will ever be cheaper than mechanical (disk). Pundits
have predicted it for decades, but it hasn't happened.
```

```

On 31-Jul-2004, robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:

> Ironically, people who claim to conserve disk space are the same ones who pad
> the record with 100 bytes 'for future expansion'

30 years ago?   I'm glad I didn't work with the same people that you worked
with.


> >   That depends ... if the element stored is mostly just presented to
> >a human viewer, or if it is used in computations.
>
> If it is used in computations, move it to working-storage.

We moved the whole record to working-storage.   We didn't trust the data
elsewhere with our systems.   Still, we worked to not have to convert data more
than we needed to, as that cost money.

Now, such internal conversions are much cheaper compared to external
conversions, so costs lead us to a different practice.


> Application programmers are amateurs out of their depth when it comes to
> managing data files. The job is better done by professionals who write
> databases.

Sometimes.   Sometimes we're competent.
```

```
programmer's salary, a manager does a manager's job and gets paid a 
manager's salary... and those who pay the salaries might wish to recall 
the warning of 'caveat emptor'.
```

```
With increasing frequency, management reallocates resources away from in-house
development, toward ERP systems such as SAP, PeopleSoft, Lawson, et al. Or it
outsources the system. Or it adopts a Formal Development Methodology designed to
tie programmers' hands. Management is giving up on trying to manage programmers.


Some argue that's a good thing. They say management should spend its time on
widget manufacturing and leave computers to Professionals.

Does it work? Are Professionally developed systems better designed, more
capable, more reliable and/or less expensive? Judging from the ones I've worked
on, the answer is No, but they allow management to ignore problems with a clear
conscience. In the old days, when workers in the trenches voiced legitimate
complaints about the systems' failings, there was pressure to fix the problem.
Now, management says 'out of my hands' or 'fill out this Change Request and go
to the back of the long line'.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

- **Message-ID:** `<411108f4.310720959@news.optonline.net>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <4110c95b.294437305@news.optonline.net>,
>Robert Wagner <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote:

>>Now, management says 'out of my hands' or 'fill out this Change Request and go
>>to the back of the long line'.
>
>Change requests?  Aren't those a remenant of Glass House computing, from 
>those 'Oldene Dayse'?

Not really. In the good old days, programming managers used them to rebuff
changes that weren't necessary or poorly advised. Nowadays, all changes need a
change request, which is reviewed and prioritized by a committee of
non-technical people who have no idea how difficult implementation would be.
Their decision is based on 'benefit to the business', fear (system will crash
without it) and politics (who supports it). As a result, easy one-line changes
cannot be made as they were in the old days.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-03T11:24:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ceol9i$1rlm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <410c13bd.106316921@news.optonline.net> <celm2e$kvv$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <410ec676.162605020@news.optonline.net> <cemicu$r01$1@panix5.panix.com> <410f4af7.196530776@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:410f4af7.196530776@news.optonline.net...
> It could have been averted with a one-sentence standard .. or a
> DATE type in Cobol.

Not sure what a "one-sentence standard" is.

Are you suggesting that the 2002 standard should have been further delayed
to allow the design of a one-size-fits-all date type?

One of the earlier changes made during the drafting of the 2002 standard was
the addition of "ACCEPT ... FROM DATE YYYYMMDD" and "ACCEPT ... FROM DAY
YYYYDDD", which many vendors (Unisys included) added as extensions to their
'85 (and, in our case, '74) implementations.

What wasn't an implementor extension was the intrinsic function amendment,
which included the current-date function, whose returned value included a
four-digit year as well as time and time zone information, and also the
necessary functions to convert the date portions of such returned values to
a standard numeric form and back again, while retaining the four-digit year.

You were aware that these "several-sizes-cover-just-about-everything" date
forms (four-digit-year calendar, four-digit-year julian,
integer-days-past-12/31/1600) have been included in the standard for COBOL
for nearly twenty years now, weren't you?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
>Not sure what a "one-sentence standard" is.

A shop standard: 'All dates will be in yyyymmdd format.'

>Are you suggesting that the 2002 standard should have been further delayed
>to allow the design of a one-size-fits-all date type?

Too late. If it had been in the '85 standard, a substantial chunk of Y2K would
have been averted. The evidence is in shops that had gone 100% database,
therefore had no Y2K problem.

>One of the earlier changes made during the drafting of the 2002 standard was
>the addition of "ACCEPT ... FROM DATE YYYYMMDD" and "ACCEPT ... FROM DAY
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>for nearly twenty years now, weren't you?

I was, but I seldom saw them used by others. Much more common was SQL:
exec sql set nls_date_format to 'yymmdd'.
             select to_char(to_date(:my-date) + 30) into :my-date from dual.
end-exec.

If there had been a date type, I believe it would have been widely used.
```

```
LW>> booklet on the preparations for Y2K...

RW> Did it say 'convert all files to database?'

   No, certainly not.

   First, they did explain all the places in the Exec (the operating  
system), and other system software, where six-digit dates could occur  
and make a problem, and where they were working on and maybe already  
had solved it (as mentioned, that is from 10 years ago).

   Then they pointed out all Exec Requests and maybe other areas of  
user written software, where Y2K-problems could arise and advised  
solutions for it.

   Coming back to databases -- not even today I would recommend to  
convert _all_ files to database.
```

```
l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:

>   Coming back to databases -- not even today I would recommend to  
>convert _all_ files to database.

I would. By taking programmers out of the data management business, it breaks
the mindset that gave us six-digit dates and packed decimal and little-endian. 

As I've said, the world of Corporate Unix -- big machines used in place of
mainframe -- is nearly 100% database. When I first saw that world, I called it a
'MIPS sink', a huge waste of CPU cycles. I've since learned to appreciate the
value of abstraction and standardization. Shops that were 100% database had NO
Y2K issues.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-04T08:57:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cer126$drr$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <410c13bd.106316921@news.optonline.net> <celm2e$kvv$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <410ec676.162605020@news.optonline.net> <cemicu$r01$1@panix5.panix.com> <410f4af7.196530776@news.optonline.net> <ceol9i$1rlm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <41100316.243672988@news.optonline.net>`

```
I wrote:

> >Are you suggesting that the 2002 standard should have been further
delayed
> >to allow the design of a one-size-fits-all date type?
>
> Too late. If it had been in the '85 standard, a substantial chunk of Y2K
would
> have been averted. The evidence is in shops that had gone 100% database,
> therefore had no Y2K problem.

Just out of curiosity:  How should *all dates* be recorded?   Stated another
way, what is the single proper format and content that is appropriate for
all contexts, environments and cultures and that COBOL should impose on all
environments?  (Hint:  there's a culture-independent date format that's been
in the standard for Quite A While Now ...)

Note that X3J4 approved the format and content of the Intrinsic Function
Amendment in January 1987, just seventeen months after they approved the
draft of the 1985 standard for submission.  The amendment went through two
public review cycles before it was approved in final form in October 1988.
It was nearly another year before ANSI approved it, but it should not have
been a surprise to anyone interested in COBOL that it was Coming Real Soon
Now as soon as the '85 standard was finished.  .

And once it was approved, it became part of that 1985 standard (as did the
Corrections Amendment a few years later).

I don't buy the idea that the availability of information in January 1987,
October 1988 or even September 1989  is Way Too Late to begin considering
its existence in the mid-to-late 1990's, where having that same information
in August 1985 is Plenty Soon Enough.

The fact that you, or the people you worked for, failed to avail yourself of
the information that COBOL compilers claiming compliance with the current
standard had been required to provide this capability starting in September
1989 should not be laid at the feet of X3J4.

> I was, but I seldom saw them used by others. Much more common was SQL:
> exec sql set nls_date_format to 'yymmdd'.
>              select to_char(to_date(:my-date) + 30) into :my-date from
dual.
> end-exec.

Ummm... which implementation of *standard* COBOL is that?   I can't find the
"EXEC" statement in the '74, '85, or '02 standards ...

> If there had been a date type, I believe it would have been widely used.

There has been, in standard COBOL, for the last fifteen years.  It's the
output format of the functions INTEGER-OF-DAY and INTEGER-OF-DATE; it's even
got its own name in the '02 standard, "integer date form".  It can represent
(and be converted to or from) any Gregorian date from 1/1/1601 to 12/31/9999
inclusive.  It can be converted to any date format, culturally-based or
otherwise, that you might wish, so long as the date falls within the above
Gregorian date range, and (aside from the range) is independent of any
particular culture.

And it didn't even take a special dedicated data type to accomplish this;
all it takes is a numeric data item (any USAGE) capable of representing a
seven-digit integer (shorter than the vaunted YYYYMMDD by one digit!).

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>Just out of curiosity:  How should *all dates* be recorded? 

In ISO 8601:1988 "Data elements and interchange formats - Information
interchange - Representation of dates and times", preferred format.

Contents would vary depending on picture:

d(4)   2004
d(6)   invalid
d(8)  20040804 (to placate the cost-savers)
d(10) 2004-08-04
d(12) 200408041245
d(17) 2004-08-04T17:45Z
d(28) 2004-08-04T12:45:30.45-05:00

Personally, I'm not fond of the hyphens and other punctuation, but it IS
universally readable without guessing and it IS an ISO standard (preferred).

>> If there had been a date type, I believe it would have been widely used.
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>seven-digit integer (shorter than the vaunted YYYYMMDD by one digit!).

Storing an "integer date form" in a file would make it Cobol-centric. Others
wouldn't be able to read it. I wouldn't be able to read it in a dump or editor.

I've used a nearly identical function since I wrote it in the '60s. I switched
to INTEGER-OF-DATE when I learned of its existance. I've never stored such a
date in a file.
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:411123c5.317587692@news.optonline.net...
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> interchange - Representation of dates and times", preferred format.

Since this standard wasn't in existence when ANSI X3.23-1985 was produced, I
wouldn't expect it to apply.  Well, you're a bit out of date.  Try ISO
8601:2000.

>
> Contents would vary depending on picture:
>

Nope.  Too many ambiguities.  ISO 8601 allows for ordinal and week dates as
well as calendar dates, and allows any number of digits in fractional
seconds, as well as truncated versions of both date and time formats.

WG4 requested of J4 that the next draft include support for current date in
ISO format*S* last June, , and a proposal has (recently) been approved that
accounts for this.  The document detailing this should be showing up on the
J4 website Real Soon Now.

"Integer date form" is retained as a single representation of dates; it is
joined by "standard numeric time form", representing seconds past midnight
(not necessarily an integer).  A large subset of the ISO 8601 date (basic
and extended calendar, ordinal and week-date formats),  time (basic and
extended local, UTC and differential formats, with or without fractional
seconds) and combined date-and-time formats are allowed as parameters to an
array of functions that provide for retrieval of values according to these
functions from the system, conversion from formatted to standard form and
back again, and testing of values against formats.  The proposal even
provides for a COBOL standard "timestamp" which combines date and time to
nanosecond precision.   It remains to be seen whether WG4 will agree to such
a comprehensive solution; this will be discussed at the WG4 meeting in The
Hague in October.

The bottom line, though, in this proposal is that for *dates* the common
underlying form from which the others are derived is the one established in
1989:  "integer date form", days past 12/31/1600.

 > Personally, I'm not fond of the hyphens and other punctuation, but it IS
> universally readable without guessing and it IS an ISO standard
(preferred).

In the ISO date proposal, as in ISO 8601, the hyphens (and colons) appear
when the format is an extended format; they don't if it's a basic format.

> Storing an "integer date form" in a file would make it Cobol-centric.
Others
> wouldn't be able to read it. I wouldn't be able to read it in a dump or
editor.

And *external* representation of something *interenally* processed has
historically involved some sort of process known as "formatting".

Note also that even the ISO date format may not be meaningful when dealing
with *locales*.

> I switched
> to INT/EGER-OF-DATE when I learned of its existance. I've never stored
such a
> date in a file.

I thought you indicated that you didn't use "integer date form" *at all*
because nobody else did.   What did I miss?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:41115b66.331830830@news.optonline.net...

> How curious that a 'standard' is ambiguous. I don't dispute what you say.
ISO
> 8601 does indeed contain a plethora of formats. Standards are supposed to
> eliminate ambiguity, not introduce it.

No, you misunderstood.  Neither ISO/IEC 1989 nor ISO 8601 is ambiguous in
this area.  What you proposed, deciding which format to use based solely on
how long the destination was, is what introduces ambiguity.  For example,
given PIC X(8) as a destination, is the date in yyyymmdd format or yyyyWwwd
format?

> >WG4 requested of J4 that the next draft include support for current date
in
> >ISO format*S* last June, , and a proposal has (recently) been approved
that
> >accounts for this.  The document detailing this should be showing up on
the
> >J4 website Real Soon Now.

> I wish J4 didn't have such a focus on current date. The issue is not
current
> date, it is dates stored in files and working-storage.

There are ten new intrinsic functions proposed for the draft standard that
are directly or peripherally involved in ISO formats.  One of these provides
current date formatted according to an ISO format.  One provides the current
local time in standard numeric time form.  None of the rest are concerned
with "current date", only with manipulating dates according to various ISO
formats.

What WG4 *asked for* was ostensibly one function.  What J4 has *provided* in
its proposal is a fairly comprehensive facility for manipulating date and
time representations into, out of, and in ISO 8601 standard formats.   Sorry
you seem to think otherwise.

> Would the format be 'nanoseconds since midnight 12/31/1600 '?

No, it wouldn't.

It's a straightforward formula that allows recovery of either of the two
arguments.

Given a value D in integer date form and a value S in standard numeric time
form, the format would be
    V = (D + (S / 100000)).

Note that given a value V in this form, the following is also true:

D = Function INTEGER-PART (V).
S = 100000 * (Function FRACTION-PART (V))
    (which is, for those who are unfamiliar with the current standard,
equivalent to
        S = 100000 * (V - Function INTEGER-PART (V))

The proposal requires a time format with fractional seconds to provide for
at least nine fractional digits (the implementor is free to provide more
precision than that) in the format and in the data.  The precision to which
"S" is defined is dependent on its container.  If S is represented as a
standard intermediate data item, the representation has 27 digits to the
right of the implied decimal point.  The accuracy of S is entirely dependent
on the accuracy (and the precision) of the value's source.

       -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:4112be7a.422744322@news.optonline.net...

> Ok, how about the obvious solution -- new picture values following the
notation
> everyone uses:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> pic yyyymmddhhmmsshh

I intended to mention explicitly in my previous response:  One of the goals
for the ISO date proposal was that it change *existing functionality as
little as possible, if at all*.   I don't see that adding the burden of all
of the PICTURE clause variations is particularly consistent with that goal.

PICTURE is already overloaded with the responsibility of defining the
length, scale factor and presence of sign for *unedited* items as well as
all of the editing responsibility and the definition of class.  This might
have been an acceptable solution in the 1968 time frame, but not now.   For
any given specification, *any* approach other than adding a new "but if it's
Tuesday ..." criterion to the interpretation of PICTURE character-strings
has to be a superior approach.   J4 rightly rejected "PICTURE X(ANY)" for
any-length data items, and the author of the date proposal rightly, in my
view, rejected any changes to PICTUREs on behalf of ISO dates formats in
favor of, well, literals containing ISO date formats and the functions
necessary to handle them.

       -Chuck Stevens
```

```
probably starting and ending, all this can be important depending on
what community and what relations is being served by your computing
center.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Calendars

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-06T13:51:11-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cf0qvv$1a2h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <ceugu1$2p8a$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <cf030e$a16$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9EKpPh29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
Extrapolating backwards from Gregorian Date January 1, 1601 to Gregorian
Date January 1, 1 is not entirely straightforward.  In the Gregorian
calendar the period October 5, 1582 through October 14, 1582 simply did not
exist, and October 15, 1582 in the Gregorian calendar is October 5, 1582 in
the Julian calendar.

This is complicated by the facts that while the Gregorian calendar was
adopted early in Europe, the Julian calendar prevailed for some time in
England and the USA, and the year changed in England on March 15 in England
and on January 1 elsewhere.  11 February 1672 in England was 21 February
1673 on the Continent; adoption of the Gregorian calendar in England in 1752
led to the omission (by decree) of September 3 through September 13 in that
year.

Then there's also the problem of calculating backwards from a starting
Gregorian date to before the omission of those days in October.  If I
understand this correctly, October 13, 1582 is a valid "Gregorian proleptic"
date.

I'm pretty sure these difficulties contributed to the decisions that
"integer date form" is defined in terms of a *starting* date of 1/1/1601 and
the arguments to the date-of-integer and day-of-integer functions are
required to be *positive* in the 2002 standard.   COBOL's intrinsic
functions aren't designed to support date values from before Gregorian Date
January 1, 1601; you've pretty much got to write your own stuff for
comparison of such dates.

    -Chuck Stevens

"Lueko Willms" <l.willms@jpberlin.de> wrote in message
news:9EKpPh29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de...

…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> in schwarzer Haut gebrandmarkt wird."     - Karl Marx     12.11.1866
```

```
In article <cf0qvv$1a2h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Calendars

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote:
> Chuck Stevens <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
> >11 February 1672 in England was 21 February
> >1673 on the Continent; adoption of the Gregorian calendar in England in
1752
> >led to the omission (by decree) of September 3 through September 13 in
that
> >year.
>
> Give us back our eleven days!

Indeed!  Consider the plight of the common laborer in England.  He was paid
by the day, but his landlord expected a full month's rent for September.
The disruption would ripple through the whole economy.  The act that
mandated the change from Old Style to New Style dates tried to address a lot
of these things, but there were still inequities.

The really weird thing, to me, was that New Year's Day used to come on March
25.  So, for example, using the old calendar, the day after March 24, 1720,
was March 25, 1721.  That would add an extra little twist to a date
calculation routine, wouldn't it?!

Walter
```

```
In article <10h871i62mdur18@corp.supernews.com>,
Walter Murray <wmurray@midtown.net> wrote:

Consider the plight of the Olde-Tyme grammar-school student in the United 
States of America.  It was easy to remember that George Washington was 
born in the year of (square root of three) times 1000... but was it 
February 11 or February 22?

>
>The really weird thing, to me, was that New Year's Day used to come on March
>25.  So, for example, using the old calendar, the day after March 24, 1720,
>was March 25, 1721.  That would add an extra little twist to a date
>calculation routine, wouldn't it?!

I recall, decades back, reading a collection of short stories by Keith 
Laumer about interrestrial relations which featured a character named 
Jamie Retief.  In one tale there was an attempt to extort something from 
the diplomatic corps under the guise that it was a local holiday, 'Double 
Gift Tuesday'.  When Retief pointed out that the local 
leader/priest/general had mentioned that yesterday was 'double-gift 
Tuesday' the creature flustered/blustered/sputtered 'Calendar Reform! Long 
overdue, now is a *week* of double-gift Tuesdays!'

Now in our days of Modern Time it seems as though there's a pretty good 
grip on the relationship folks would like to see between the Heavenly Bowl 
above and the pieces of paper torn of a series of one-panel cartoons 
below; there hasn't been a calendar 'crisis' in a goodly long while... I 
try to keep the memory of such things alive, though, by conversations 
like:

'Well... it's Monday again.'

'Aye, happens every week around this time, too... and a Good Thing it is, 
or else I'd have to rewrite all my date routines.'

DD
```

```
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>year.

This points out that what programmers call Julian Dates have nothing whatever to
do with the Julian Calendar. They misused the word Julian in an attempt to
legitimize a lame format that was never used before the '60s.

Russia didn't use the Gregorian calendar until 1918. It then switched to several
'progressive' calendars until WWII. The Russian Orthodox Church still uses
Julian, which explains why Christmas in Russia is celebrated on Jan 7.
```

```
According to articles I've read -- I don't offhand remember whether it was
in Bible Review or Biblical Archaeology Review -- there is strong evidence
that the tradition of Christmas as January 7 significantly predates the
calendar shift (and perhaps even the Great Eastern Schism itself).

throughout the Orthodox Communion, not limited merely to the Autocephalous
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Calendars

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-09T11:31:19-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cf8fto$cnv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <ceugu1$2p8a$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <cf030e$a16$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9EKpPh29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <cf0qvv$1a2h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <4114367c.519017221@news.optonline.net> <cf82nd$3oh$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <4117ad6c.13098690@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:4117ad6c.13098690@news.optonline.net...

> It's more likely Christmas was celebrated on December 25 under the Julian
> calendar. Orthodox translated that to the 29th day of the fourth month in
the
> Coptic calendar. When the rest of the world went Gregorian, Orthodox
continued
> using the *old* date, which is now January 7 Gregorian.

What I'm trying to say is that there is some evidence for Christmas being
celebrated on January 7 -- or January 6 or January 19 -- long before the
establishment of the Gregorian calendar.

*In Rome*, Christmas was celebrated on December 25 as early as 355AD, and it
is likely this date was chosen to coincide with the winter solstice.  Though
some authors assert that this became universal throughout Christendom by the
late fourth century -- mostly those who presume Roman primacy in Christendom
even when the seat of the Church was in
Byzantium/Constantinople/Instanbul -- there appear to have been those whose
ecclesiastical traditions do not follow that convention that didn't
celebrate the *nativity* at all, and when they did begin to do so (not under
the influence of Rome) they included it as part of Theophany on (Julian
calendar) January 6 (not 7), and do so to this day.

You are welcome to presume that the date of Christmas was first established
and accepted worldwide as December 25, and any and all divergences from that
date stem from the establishment of a new calendar in 1582 by Gregory XIII.

As for me, I'd prefer to accept the word of historians, archaeologists, and
theologians who have studied the subject in some depth and have concluded
that there's a whole lot more to it than the actions of Gregory XIII in 1582
and the side-effects of those actions.   The assertion that the *earliest
and traditional* date for the celebration of Christmas is January 6 (Julian
calendar) comes from an Orthodox source and reflects the celebration of the
Nativity in conjunction with the Theophany.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Calendars

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
>Nativity in conjunction with the Theophany.

We in the West think celebrating Christmas is synonymous with opening gifts.
Historically, Christ's birth and the 'revelation' that he was divine were two
different events and holidays. 

Traditionally, Christmas was a period of time rather than a single day, hence
the phrase 'twelve days of Christmas'. It began following Advent and ended the
day before Epiphany aka Theophany. Christmas celebrated Christ's birth or
nativity; Epiphany celebrated a visit by three wise 'kings' bearing gifts,
therefore the day when we open gifts. 

In the West, Christmas lasted from December 25 through January 5 followed by
gift opening Epiphany on January 6. In the Orthodox East, Christmas lasted from
January 7 (December 25 Julian) thru January 18 followed by gift opening
Theophany on January 19. 

The problem was, some retailers and all individuals ended their fiscal year in
December. It was untidy to have the orgy of materialism spilling over to the
following year, so we fixed the commercial problem by moving gift-giving to the
first day of Christmas.
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:41186397.59741450@news.optonline.net...

> We in the West think celebrating Christmas is synonymous with opening
gifts.

Not clear that that's been a universal custom since apostolic days, however.

> Historically, Christ's birth and the 'revelation' that he was divine were
two
> different events and holidays.

In some societies.  Some orthodox claim that the *nativity* wasn't
originally celebrated at all, Theophany being much the more important
holiday, and the nativity commemoration evolving out of, and remaining
secondary to, that of Theophany.

> Traditionally, Christmas was a period of time rather than a single day,
hence
> the phrase 'twelve days of Christmas'.

Not universally.

>  It began following Advent ...

Circular definition, applicable only to Western churches.  Nota bene
"ad-venire", approximately "to arrive", "to come to [a place]".  Defining
anything in terms of "Advent" dismisses the Orthodox tradition altogether,
and the Roman Catholic encyclopedia flatly describes Advent as being
celebrated *in the Western church*.

>  and ended the
> day before Epiphany aka Theophany.

Later, in some locations, maybe.

> Christmas celebrated Christ's birth or
> nativity; Epiphany celebrated a visit by three wise 'kings' bearing gifts,
> therefore the day when we open gifts.

The first recorded celebrations of the Epiphany seem to be about
commemorating Jesus' *baptism*.

> In the West, Christmas lasted from December 25 through January 5 followed
by
> gift opening Epiphany on January 6.

In some cultures.

In the Orthodox East, Christmas lasted from
> January 7 (December 25 Julian) thru January 18 followed by gift opening
> Theophany on January 19.

In some Orthodox denominations.

> The problem was, some retailers and all individuals ended their fiscal
year in
> December.

???    Yeah, I bet Arthur Anderson was really after those merchants
supplying goods to the families of the Crusaders to get their books closed
on time!

> It was untidy to have the orgy of materialism spilling over to the
> following year, so we fixed the commercial problem by moving gift-giving
to the
> first day of Christmas.

I was under the *distinct* impression that the "orgy of materialism" aspect
of Christmas was a phenomenon that had blossomed primarily in the last
couple of centuries.   I don't think gift-giving, or its abuse, has been a
consistent practice throughout all of Christianity for the last two
millennia; in fact, I strongly suspect where it has been adopted in the East
it has been through imitation of the West, just as gift-giving at Chanukkah
is sometimes practiced in imitation of Christianity (the "Chanukkah bush"
being an even more egregious example of such imitation).
    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
""" ----------------
The answer lies in the pagan origins of Christmas. In ancient Babylon,
```

- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <ceugu1$2p8a$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <cf030e$a16$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9EKpPh29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <cf0qvv$1a2h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <4114367c.519017221@news.optonline.net> <cf82nd$3oh$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <4117ad6c.13098690@news.optonline.net> <cf8fto$cnv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <41186397.59741450@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408101506.20a4d2c@posting.google.com>`

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0408101506.20a4d2c@posting.google.com...

> The feast, the gift giving, carolling, the Yule logs, holly,
> mistletoe, decorated trees, the date of a couple of days after winter
> solstace were _ALL_ pre-christian and appropriated to be recast as if
> they had christian significance or even origin.

Moreover, among these, at least the Yule logs (Scandinavian) holly (English)
and decorated trees (German, and not particularly popular outside of that
culture until Prince Al set one up in 1841 for Vicky and the kids) are
decidedly Germanic customs.

Gift giving appears to have been a *Roman* custom absorbed from the feast of
Saturnalia, not necessarily widespread everywhere or particularly early, and
rationalized much later in the church because of the example of the Magi,
and later still because of the example of St. Nicholas.

All of these were *seriously* frowned upon by the early Church as of pagan
origin.

Caroling as a custom is *distinctly* English, but aside from its connections
with the medieval city waites, I'm not yet convinced it was fundamentally
pagan (as distinct from secular) in origin.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
You may think so, but it can be found as equally traditional in other
countries including:

"""
The carolling tradition, which is of pagan origin, lived on up to this
century. Especially in the south-western parts of Finland, the songs
performed by carolling young men included Tapanin virsi. The song
remained part of the Boxing Day (Feast of St Stephen) ceremony: young
men went from house to house, one dressed as Tapani in a straw suit,
others as Christmas bears, billy goats or cranes. They sang and danced
Tapanin virsi, ending with a request for beer and food for their own
feast.
"""
```

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0408102245.3099f602@posting.google.com...

> The carolling tradition, which is of pagan origin, lived on up to this
> century. Especially in the south-western parts of Finland, the songs
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> feast.

Interesting point.  The earliest historical date I can find for Finland is
the first Finnish crusade in 1155, and one of the leaders of this Crusade
was an English bishop named Henry, later apparently canonized and made the
patron saint of Finland (his companion Erik is the patron saint of Sweden).
Henry was killed in 1156, so it's unlikely he took the custom back to
England from Finland, and if he was bent on *Christianizing* Finland he
would hardly be likely to *introduce* fundamentally pagan practices.

The Scandinavians that seemed to "intermix" with the English during the Dark
Ages seemed to be speakers of Germanic languages, whereas what we now know
as south-west Finland spoke a member of the Finno-Ugric group, which isn't
even part of the Indo-European family.

Did this custom evolve *entirely* independently in Finland and England, or
was it borrowed by one from the other, and if the latter, in which
direction?

Stated a different way, is there *a* caroling tradition, or are there
*multiple* caroling traditions that happen to resemble each other?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
RW> In the West, Christmas lasted from December 25 through January 5
RW> followed by gift opening Epiphany on January 6. In the Orthodox East,
RW> Christmas lasted from January 7 (December 25 Julian) thru January 18
RW> followed by gift opening Theophany on January 19.

   This reminds me of the Coptic-Ethiopian and the old Persian, i.e.
Jalali calendar which solved the problem of the irregularities of the
solar and lunar cylces and the difficulties of dividing the solar year
into cycles of days related to the moon cycle in a very simple way:

   they had 12 months of equal length, namely 30 days each, plus an
extra period of 5 extra days at the end of the year. In a leap year,
one could simply add 1 extra day to this special period.

   I would like this calendar to be reintroduced.


Yours,

/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote 

> According to articles I've read -- I don't offhand remember whether it was
> in Bible Review or Biblical Archaeology Review -- there is strong evidence
> that the tradition of Christmas as January 7 significantly predates the

Christianity took over existing festivals and celebrations and renamed
them and/or reinvented them for its own purposes.  Easter, for
example, is the spring festival of the Godess of Fertility - hence
eggs and rabbits which have no christian connection at all.

In northern and western europe, a few days after the Solstace, after
it had been detected and preparations done, the need for the supplies
that will last to the harvest could be calculated and the excess
eaten.  The midwinter feast in celebration of the return of the sun,
was corrupted to the 'son'.

In southern europe this was less important and the christians had to
take over different local ceremonies which is why christmas is quite
different in orthodox churches and easter is more important.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>In northern and western europe, a few days after the Solstace, after
>it had been detected and preparations done, the need for the supplies
>that will last to the harvest could be calculated and the excess
>eaten.  The midwinter feast in celebration of the return of the sun,
>was corrupted to the 'son'.

On that note, the Islamic 'Hijri' calendar is based strictly on the moon. A
month, averaging 29.53 days, begins when some church authority SEES the crescent
after a new moon. Although the orbit of the moon can be calculated with great
accuracy, Islam doesn't believe it will return until someone sees it. As a
result, neighboring countries may be off plus or minus a day due to vagaries of
weather. In Persian Gulf countries, Hijri is the official calendar for secular
as well as religious purposes. The calendar is based on the Qur'an (Sura IX,
36-37) and its proper observance is a sacred duty for Muslims. 

The enlightened Saudi Arabia is an exception. Since 1999 it has used the
calculated position of the moon rather than relying on observation. In other
Gulf countries, it's not possible to print next year's, or even next month's,
calendar in advance.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

> Christianity took over existing festivals and celebrations and renamed
> them and/or reinvented them for its own purposes.

In some parts of the world, the Roman Catholic Church has been *very*
adaptable to local customs and traditions. Mexico is a good example.
There are a number of local saints plus "Day of the Dead" takes place
on All Saint's Day. In Brazil, New Year celebrations include floating
offerings to "Imeja" out to sea.

Of course cultural evolution is ongoing and is really quite
fascinating. In years past the local PBS station ran TV college
courses in the early morning. One of these was a series on
anthropology. A particular episode featured the inhabitants of the
Trobriand islands (well known from the work of Branislaw Malinowski).

1. When the British took over, they introduced the locals to cricket.
Yearly cricket matches have replaced (partly ritualistic) warfare
between clans.

2. Some of the elaborate dances involved in the ceremonies that take
place before the games resemble airplanes flying and landing - a
reference to the "cargo cults" that developed during WWII.

3. In spite of attempts by missionaries to wipe it out, the islanders
retained extremely suggestive dances which included rhythmic pelvic
movements and chants which were somewhat euphemisticly translated to
"I like .... my thing".

[Wondering what lasting imprint COBOL will leave on *our* culture?]
```

```
.     Am  06.08.04
 schrieb  charles.stevens@unisys.com (Chuck Stevens)
     bei  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
      in  cf0qvv$1a2h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com
   ueber  Re: Calendars

CS> Extrapolating backwards from Gregorian Date January 1, 1601 to
CS> Gregorian Date January 1, 1 is not entirely straightforward.  In
the
CS> Gregorian calendar the period October 5, 1582 through October 14,
CS> 1582 simply did not exist, and October 15, 1582 in the Gregorian
CS> calendar is October 5, 1582 in the Julian calendar.

   Sure, it does not make sense to use the Gregorian calendar as Day,
Month, Year where and when it was not valid. There is not even a sense
of using the Julian calendar before it was introduced by this monk in,
I think, the 7th century A.D.

   The point is to have a fixed day number, counting days instead of
representing them as days in various cyclical entities, since using
such a fixed day number, one can calculate those representations in
various calendars.

   The book which I mentioned, by Edward M. Reingold and NAchum
Dershowitz, "Calendrical Calculations" (Cambridge University Press;>
see http://www.calendarists.com) presents such formulae and
algorithms; it also comes with a CD-ROM where all these can be found
as LISP source code.

CS> I'm pretty sure these difficulties contributed to the decisions
that
CS> "integer date form" is defined in terms of a starting date of
CS> 1/1/1601 and the arguments to the date-of-integer and
day-of-integer
CS> functions are required to be positive in the 2002 standard.

   Others, like some spreadsheet programs, use a date in 1900 as day 1
for their fixed day numbers. Astronomers use the Julian day number,
the number of that day within a certain astronomical cycle of 7980
years, and this Julian day number for today is 2'453'225, well
actually only after noon, since the Julian day is counted from noon to
noon, and not from midnight to midnight.

CS> COBOL's intrinsic functions aren't designed to support date values
CS> from before Gregorian Date January 1, 1601; you've pretty much got
to
CS> write your own stuff for comparison of such dates.

   That's OK since the built-in functions in COBOL only support the
Gregorian calendar.

   Reingold and Dershowitz wanted to show algorithms for all known
calendars, wanted to avoid negative numbers for large parts of our
recorded history, use less digits than the Julian day number, and use
a sensible date as epoch, i.e. as day number 1. So they used the
fictional January 1, year 1. All other fixed day numbers can be
derived from it by simple addition or subtraction.

   Today's R.D. is 731'800. The R.D. fixed day number for the epoch of
the COBOL serial day number is 584'755; I understand that this January
1, 1601 Gregorian is serial day number 1 for COBOL; thus one would
only have to add 584'754 to it, and can then use the algorithms
presented by Reingold and Dershowitz without further changes.

   Today's COBOL serial day number would be 147'046, right?

    [the original message was written on Saturday, August  7 ]]

Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.mlwerke.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

"Das Volk, das ein anderes Volk unterjocht, schmiedet seine eigenen
```

```
   ueber  Re: Calendars

CS> Extrapolating backwards from Gregorian Date January 1, 1601 to
CS> Gregorian Date January 1, 1 is not entirely straightforward.  In
the
CS> Gregorian calendar the period October 5, 1582 through October 14,
CS> 1582 simply did not exist, and October 15, 1582 in the Gregorian
CS> calendar is October 5, 1582 in the Julian calendar.

   Sure, it does not make sense to use the Gregorian calendar as Day,
Month, Year where and when it was not valid. There is not even a sense
of using the Julian calendar before it was introduced by this monk in,
I think, the 7th century A.D.

   The point is to have a fixed day number, counting days instead of
representing them as days in various cyclical entities, since using
such a fixed day number, one can calculate those representations in
various calendars.

   The book which I mentioned, by Edward M. Reingold and NAchum
Dershowitz, "Calendrical Calculations" (Cambridge University Press;>
see http://www.calendarists.com) presents such formulae and
algorithms; it also comes with a CD-ROM where all these can be found
as LISP source code.

CS> I'm pretty sure these difficulties contributed to the decisions
that
CS> "integer date form" is defined in terms of a starting date of
CS> 1/1/1601 and the arguments to the date-of-integer and
day-of-integer
CS> functions are required to be positive in the 2002 standard.

   Others, like some spreadsheet programs, use a date in 1900 as day 1
for their fixed day numbers. Astronomers use the Julian day number,
the number of that day within a certain astronomical cycle of 7980
years, and this Julian day number for today is 2'453'225, well
actually only after noon, since the Julian day is counted from noon to
noon, and not from midnight to midnight.

CS> COBOL's intrinsic functions aren't designed to support date values
CS> from before Gregorian Date January 1, 1601; you've pretty much got
to
CS> write your own stuff for comparison of such dates.

   That's OK since the built-in functions in COBOL only support the
Gregorian calendar.

   Reingold and Dershowitz wanted to show algorithms for all known
calendars, wanted to avoid negative numbers for large parts of our
recorded history, use less digits than the Julian day number, and use
a sensible date as epoch, i.e. as day number 1. So they used the
fictional January 1, year 1. All other fixed day numbers can be
derived from it by simple addition or subtraction.

   Today's R.D. is 731'800. The R.D. fixed day number for the epoch of
the COBOL serial day number is 584'755; I understand that this January
1, 1601 Gregorian is serial day number 1 for COBOL; thus one would
only have to add 584'754 to it, and can then use the algorithms
presented by Reingold and Dershowitz without further changes.

   Today's COBOL serial day number would be 147'046, right?

    [the original message was written on Saturday, August  7 ]]
```

```
ISO 8601 doesn't seem to specify anything about "other calendars", but
ISO/IEC 9945-2 apparently does, and t_fmt and d_fmt as they relate to
locale-dates from that standard are explicitly accounted for in the current
COBOL standard (ISO/IEC 1989:2002 pages 64-65, 8.2, Locales).

Stated a different way, I haven't personally worked with "other calendars",
but the mechanisms are certainly there in standard COBOL to do so.

    -Chuck Stevens

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote in message news:<4112be7a.422744322@news.optonline.net>...

> Ok, how about the obvious solution -- new picture values following the
> notation everyone uses:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Another reason using PICTUREs as the mechanism whereby to describe ISO
date formats in COBOL might not be the best idea in the long run:

The proposal for the *initial* ISO date/time design limits the format
parameters to non-numeric or national *literals*.  However, that
proposal is specifically written to allow for the extension of that
mechanism to include formats in data items, variable at execution
time, as an enhancement in a future standard or revision.

If ISO date/time formats were to be described using PICTURE
character-strings, it would preclude forever the possibility of
variable formats, unless and until PICTURE character-strings that were
*variable at execution time* were also included.

Out of curiosity, did you *really* think the idea of using PICTUREs to
describe ISO date and time formats *all the way* through?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-12T08:45:59-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cfg3bn$2gbs$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<410ec676.162605020@news.optonline.net> <cemicu$r01$1@panix5.panix.com> <410f4af7.196530776@news.optonline.net> <ceol9i$1rlm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <41100316.243672988@news.optonline.net> <cer126$drr$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <411123c5.317587692@news.optonline.net> <cerbcj$l0b$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <41115b66.331830830@news.optonline.net> <cetnag$286e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <4112be7a.422744322@news.optonline.net> <c60a0b82.0408110905.1c33ecb8@posting.google.com> <389lh0dupl6am62n352kd46bc5f130php2@4ax.com>`

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message
news:389lh0dupl6am62n352kd46bc5f130php2@4ax.com...

> What's this about variable? There's nothing variable about yyyymmdd.
> It's an eight byte date that is universally understood.

Note that the formats are case sensitive in the proposal because they are
case sensitive in ISO 8601, and the proposal covers *only* formats described
in that standard, not formats that might be described in a future version of
ISO 8601 that might provide for case-insensitivity.

When the format in ISO 8601 shows an upper-case character (Y, M, D, W),
COBOL expects an upper-case character.  Where the format in ISO 8601 shows a
lower-case character (w, h, m, s), COBOL expects a lower-case character.
Alphanumeric and national *literals* are case-sensitive in COBOL.

EC-ARGUMENT-FUNCTION can be expected during execution of any function except
TEST-FORMATTED-DATETIME if case is not observed carefully in the composition
of the format parameter to that function.

Now, to your question:

The current proposal allows this:
    77  pic-x-date-1 PICTURE X(8).
    77  pic-x-date-2 PICTURE X(8).
    MOVE CURRENT-FORMATTED-DATE ("YYYYMMDD")) TO pic-x-date-1.
    MOVE CURRENT-FORMATTED-DATE ("YYYYWwwD") TO pic-x-date-2.

The proposal *doesn't* allow this:

    77  some-format PICTURE X(8).
    77  pic-x-date-1 PICTURE X(8).
    77  pic-x-date-2 PICTURE X(8).
    MOVE "YYYYMMDD" TO some-format.
    MOVE CURRENT-FORMATTED-DATE (some-format) TO pic-x-date-1.
    MOVE "YYYYWwwD" TO some-format.
    MOVE CURRENT-FORMATTED-DATE (some-format) TO pic-x-date-2.

but I expect some implementors will allow it, and it can certainly be
considered for a future enhancement to standard COBOL.

Using "yyyymmdd" as a format -- either as the argument to the function call
in the first example above, or as the source of the move just before the
function call in the hypothetical implementation-dependent second example,
would cause the function call to encounter EC-ARGUMENT-FUNCTION.   So would
using "YYYYWWWD" in either case.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
And if century is unneeded, then you have wasted two bytes.  Memory

programming and the shelf life for most software was supposed to be
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ The Great Date Debate [Was Re: Layout Hell-o]

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net>
- **Date:** 2004-08-14T16:58:00-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<HVvTc.15858$5s3.12357@fe40.usenetserver.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<pm52h0d67rppn8u2n8v33sk4cbtrvhvfkf@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <410c13bd.106316921@news.optonline.net> <celm2e$kvv$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <410ec676.162605020@news.optonline.net> <cemicu$r01$1@panix5.panix.com> <410f4af7.196530776@news.optonline.net> <ceol9i$1rlm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <41100316.243672988@news.optonline.net> <pm52h0d67rppn8u2n8v33sk4cbtrvhvfkf@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:

> On Wed, 04 Aug 2004 00:09:03 GMT, robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert
> Wagner) enlightened us:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> And if century is unneeded, then you have wasted two bytes.

The problem is that no one can look into the future and say "we'll never 
need the century!"  Right now, there is a set of programs I recently 
inherited (due to internal reorganization).  In the main record for that 
subsystem, there is an "estimated delivery date" in the format YDDD. 
The thought behind that was "oh, we'll never exceed 30 days or so, so 
this will be fine."

Well, this record in involved in an interface with another system, which 
has proven to be less than 100%.  In fact, the reason it wasn't expanded 
with the rest of the Y2K stuff is that the interfacing system didn't see 
the need to do it.  Well, now we have programs (both in development and 
production) that are timing out because of the length of the chain it 
has to walk on these records.  There are some that are over 10 years 
old, and the only way we have of knowing is the date stamp in the record.

Recent analysis showed that some of these up to three years old are 
accurately showing as still open (a resync between both systems 
demonstrated this).  Now, I'm sure that the original designer thought 
they were being "efficient", especially when this saved a whole 
character per transaction.  However, they have been shown to be very 
short-sighted, as their original design is now being stretched past its 
limitation.

Mr. Wagner had a point a few posts back (yes, I'm way behind - found out 
we're expecting #3, and life has been crazy for the last few weeks) that 
programmers had seemingly not learned their lesson from Y2K, and were 
still trying to be "efficient" at the expense of flexibility of future 
use.  All he got in response were pedantic replies, but he has a very 
valid point.  In today's increasingly networked culture, there is 
apparent folly by continuing to use proprietary "packed" formats (and 
other such techniques) on external data stores.  I realize that existing 
designs may not be able to be redesigned (as I'm still trying to figure 
out a way around my single-year dilemma).  But for new development (as 
indicated by Mr. Klein's analysis), instantiating this is just asking 
for trouble down the road.

The key to this is how the data is exposed.  I really like the concept 
of the "integer of date" format.  It seems to me to be no different from 
most relational databases - they don't store the date in YYYY-MM-DD 
HH:MM:SS.PPPPPP format, but they _expose_ it to the outside world that 
way (or lots of different ways, depending on how you ask for it or how 
you configure it).  Without hex-editing the database's files, you can't 
see the date in its "native" format.  While you wouldn't dream of 
sending a database repository to someone else (unless they had the exact 
same version of software, but even then, it would be flaky), people 
routinely try to exchange files created and maintained by COBOL programs 
to people whose tools are often no more sophisticated that Notepad.

Ponder this...  A programmer decides to use display usage on a 
particular file.  A few years on down the road, two mergers later, a new 
manager decides they need to send that file to an external entity.  Is 
the system viewed as sluggish and inefficient, or responsive and flexible?

Remember - it's true that Mr. Wagner cannot see into the future - but 
neither can any of the rest of either.  For new development, in my 
opinion, usage display (in files) or relational databases are the way to 
go.  They provide the greatest flexibility for future (unknown) 
requirements.
```

```
>inherited (due to internal reorganization).  In the main record for that 
>subsystem, there is an "estimated delivery date" in the format YDDD. 
>The thought behind that was "oh, we'll never exceed 30 days or so, so 
>this will be fine."

This was similar to a problem Mr Hamasaki found in a system that was using 
single-digit years... it blew up the first time it tried to span a decade.

>
>Well, this record in involved in an interface with another system, which 
>has proven to be less than 100%.  In fact, the reason it wasn't expanded 
>with the rest of the Y2K stuff is that the interfacing system didn't see 
>the need to do it.

Gah... systems do not see needs, *people* do.

[snip]

>Now, I'm sure that the original designer thought 
>they were being "efficient", especially when this saved a whole 
>character per transaction.  However, they have been shown to be very 
>short-sighted, as their original design is now being stretched past its 
>limitation.

Bingo.  '... their original design is now being stretched past its 
limitation.'  Consider:  two people get married and buy a compact car; 
this carries them fine.  Next a child comes along, the car can still carry 
them and their groceries... no sweat.  Then comes along another child... 
what might seem to be a reasonable conclusion?

A reasonable conclusion is that either you get a new car or resign 
yourself to making a lot of extra trips, in essence you redesign the 
solution to your transportation needs.  Note that this instance is not one 
of timespan but of 'system volume'... but the conclusion is the same; 
needs have changed to the point where redesign is necessary.

The original system's design might have exceeded the original system's 
requirements; it is not the original system's fault that someone decided 
to try to accomodate the needs of a multi-child family with a compact car.

[snip]

>Ponder this...  A programmer decides to use display usage on a 
>particular file.  A few years on down the road, two mergers later, a new 
>manager decides they need to send that file to an external entity.  Is 
>the system viewed as sluggish and inefficient, or responsive and flexible?

Likewise, ponder this: a couple gets married and immediately buys the 
largest minivan that's on the market... after all, a few years down the 
line they might have five kids and two Dobermans to ferry about, you never 
knoooo-ooooowwww.

Needs are evaluated and extrapolated... and systems are designed and 
implemented.  If you then try to fit more folks into a compact car than it 
was designed for then the results wind up looking like clowns at a circus.

DD
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

> In article <HVvTc.15858$5s3.12357@fe40.usenetserver.com>,
> LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> mainfested as a crooned 'you never knoooo-oooooowwww!'.

Does that in any way change its truth?  This isn't just some mindless 
suggestion, though - it's based on lessons learned throughout several 
years of software design.  At least where I work (and I've heard this 
taught in class as well), my job as a software developer is to read the 
requirements from the user and try to decided what they are _really_ 
asking for.

I recently reviewed a requirement for a "switch" for an interface.  I 
asked the question - just because we have a switch for another 
interface, does that mean we need one for this one?  I was told, in 
effect, to shut up and color, but it's my job to completely understand a 
requirement, so I can provide a system that is responsive to their 
current needs, and flexible for perceived future needs.

>>Right now, there is a set of programs I recently 
>>inherited (due to internal reorganization).  In the main record for that 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Gah... systems do not see needs, *people* do.

Okay - "the user community for the interfacing system didn't see the 
need to change their system."

>>Now, I'm sure that the original designer thought 
>>they were being "efficient", especially when this saved a whole 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> what might seem to be a reasonable conclusion?

Either buy a bigger vehicle (sedan, mini-van, SUV, etc.), or rent a 
larger vehicle for times when they need more space.

> A reasonable conclusion is that either you get a new car or resign 
> yourself to making a lot of extra trips, in essence you redesign the 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> to try to accomodate the needs of a multi-child family with a compact car.

Right.  But it is _my_ fault if I don't learn from that, and make my 
designs for future systems more accommodating.  And, although it's not 
the other system's "fault" per se, I still believe I'm justified in 
calling the design short-sighted.  I can't believe they didn't have 
problems with this during the 1989/1990 rollover or the 1999/2000 
rollover.  The fact is, they did, but no one (on their end) saw it as a 
big enough deal to justify a software change.

>>Ponder this...  A programmer decides to use display usage on a 
>>particular file.  A few years on down the road, two mergers later, a new 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> knoooo-ooooowwww.

By the time the fifth child comes along, that vehicle will probably have 
deteriorated to the point that they need to buy a new one.  However, 
software isn't quite like a car.  Maybe they could pay an extra $2K up 
front for their compact car, with the understanding that if/when the 
time ever arose that they needed seating for 7, the seats and storage 
room would be there with no additional cash outlay.  Bits don't 
deteriorate, and especially with relational databases, you can carry 
around extra capability without it being harder to get into a standard 
parking space.

> Needs are evaluated and extrapolated... and systems are designed and 
> implemented.  If you then try to fit more folks into a compact car than it 
> was designed for then the results wind up looking like clowns at a circus.

If you see software design as having to be as rigid as the physical 
constraints of a vehicle, then I can see how you would feel that way. 
Software developers in 2004 have one thing that the software developers 
in 1974 didn't have - an extra thirty years of industry experience. 
There are several lessons that one could learn from those 30 years.

One of the biggest lessons, in my opinion, is that systems are often 
asked to do things well outside of the narrow scope of their original 
requirements.  Part of this is due to a lack of understanding between 
the system procurers and the designers (often traced back to unclear 
requirements).  Part of it is also due to people using automated systems 
and beginning to visualize other ways the system could meet their needs. 
  As a designer/developer, I feel that I would be doing a disservice to 
my employer/client if I do not apply that knowledge, and design a system 
that is flexible enough to do what they initially ask of it, and maybe 
just a little bit more - *especially* when it comes to dates.

An example - I was asked to design and code software to allow a golf 
"association" to track its members, sign them up for a tournament, place 
the people into teams, calculate handicaps, score, and calculate payouts 
for top scorers for each day, top scorer within skill level (A/B/C/D), 
and overall top scorer.  By adding a single foreign key to a few tables, 
this application supports more than one organization in its _initial_ 
state.  And, with a simple tweak, if organizations ever wish to share 
members, a new table (initially populated with values from the current 
Members table) would do it quite quickly.

Was I asked to make a multi-organization-supporting system?  Not 
initially.  After the first tournament, the customer asked me what it 
would take to get other golfing organizations loaded into it.  Sounds 
like I hit the nail on the head with that one.  :)  (Of course, there 
was a little self-serving in that as well - I had hoped that they would 
give me rights to the program, and they have.  Now, _my_ job is easier!)
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
```
