But you can make an educated guess.  Is it smart to make all your dates 
also have an associated time, down to 18 digits of precision?  Probably 
not.  Is it a good idea to store off four digits for a year?  In my 
opinion, that answer is yes.  If you answer no (and have a 
requirements-based reason for answering no), you don't have a faulty 
design - you just have an inflexible one.

>>This isn't just some mindless 
>>suggestion, though - it's based on lessons learned throughout several 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Mean) command.

Which makes this job such fun.  :)  I'm glad I can still keep my fingers 
in the code - if I had to go straight design, I'd probably go insane. 
(Granted, that probably wouldn't be much of a trip...)

>>I recently reviewed a requirement for a "switch" for an interface.  I 
>>asked the question - just because we have a switch for another 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> talking about it... so maybe it'll do... *something* over here, too!'

Well, I suspected it to be along the lines of "A", and I was correct. 
However, the interfacing system has the potential to process classified 
information; our is unclassified.  So, the requirement exists to "turn 
the interface on and off" - but we did get them to agree to let us make 
the decision on exactly how (the behind the scenes part) we would do that.

>>I was told, in 
>>effect, to shut up and color, but it's my job to completely understand a 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> you've taken upon yourself?

Thanks...  :)  And, it's not in a written description, but it's expected 
of each of us.  My current project spans a couple of things - first, the 
USAF's decision to change the F-22 to an A/F-22, and second, the USN's 
decision to track some stuff from the CV-22 in our system.  Right now, 
our code is littered with checks like "IF MDS = '  F022A A' " or "IF MDS 
= '  F0119100' " (that's the engine it uses) because they track their 
parts differently from any existing aircraft in the USAF inventory.

The reason they were allowed to do this is that the regulation has 
changed, and new aircraft have to follow the new standard.  Enter the 
CV-22 - they use the same type of stuff as the F-22.  So, this project 
entails pulling out all these checks, creating a lookup table, and 
having the checks look at the table.  Now, the original F-22 design was 
complete (it works, and satisfied the requirement), but it was very 
short-sighted.  Quite expensive to go and make the change, but when the 
Joint Strike Fighter comes along, it'll be much cheaper and much faster, 
as the design is flexible enough to drop other yet-to-be-determined 
aircraft into it.

(In fact, now that the "MDS" is now " AF022A A", the original code 
doesn't even work.  In the new design, that's a one-row change in a 
relational table - total cost to the taxpayers of this great land, $.05 
(or somewhere thereabout).)

…[12 more quoted lines elided]…

Okay - but for some reason, the chain of command doesn't like hearing 
that sort of stuff.  Their users complain when the program times out, 
and it's our fault because we *still* haven't fixed it, yet we can't get 
the interfacing system's managers to make the change.  I can't believe 
it would be _that_ complicated of a change - they're in the same 
environment our system is in.

it).  What is the parallel for "renting a larger vehicle" in the 
software design world?  (As an aside, we already do the multiple-trip 
thing with this interface.  The packets have a maximum size of around 
3,000 characters, and they send us 80-character lines even when a CR/LF 
will do.  So, we sometimes get as many as 4 or 5 responses for one 
transaction.

(Yes, I asked to inherit this interface - I'm making it my goal to 
streamline it and make it more usable.  There are programs that people 
in the field have stopped using because they just don't work.)

this particular case, a second digit would help us immensely.  But, it 
would still violate the standard that we have in every other place in 
our database of storing a 4-digit year.

(What I also learned is that I have an opportunity to be really creative 
and come up with a self-sliding 4-year window.  I think I'll probably do

>>However,

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Yes, but everything in a car is tangible.  People don't expect their car 
to have a pop-up calendar from which they can select dates, but they are 
happy with the little sticker that JiffyLube puts on their windshield 
reminding them when their next oil change is due, or the "time for a 
tune-up" light that turns from green to yellow to red (on Hondas).

But, give someone a web page with a required date input field, and 
people all of a sudden can't type 2004-08-16 (or 8/16/2004, or 
16/8/2004) - "I've got to have a pop-up box where I click a block, and 
it fills in the date for me."

Of course, talking about a car, there are those who attempt to push cars 
beyond their designed limits - you usually hear about them on the news, 
though.  Not the same with software...  :)

>>Maybe they could pay an extra $2K up 
>>front for their compact car, with the understanding that if/when the 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Big 3 office's code.

True.  I guess that's where "knowing your customer" is very important. 
As a USAF member, the USAF's interest in my work is making sure I 
produce the longest-lasting, flexible, yet efficient, accurate, and 
stable software I can.  Now, a contractor may say something along the 
lines of "well, this is what they asked for - I think they may need this 
too, but I'll just do this really well and maybe I'll get the contract 
when they figure out that they need this too."

I don't have that luxury.  In fact, I've been asked a few times why I've 
done things a certain way, and had to explain myself.  The answers I 
gave that delivered the least satisfaction are the ones that start with 
"well, I was told to..."

>>>Needs are evaluated and extrapolated... and systems are designed and 
>>>implemented.  If you then try to fit more folks into a compact car than it 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> transactions through your Pentium and see if it bogs.

Heck, my Athlon locks up with Folding@home...  :)  We're looking a 
2M/day (which, I realize, is small potatoes compared with some other 
systems), which is more than we've ever thrown at the base-level version 
of the system.

>>Software developers in 2004 have one thing that the software developers 
>>in 1974 didn't have - an extra thirty years of industry experience. 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> days it was done better then than now.

Not those lessons - yes, looking back at software development from the 
1950s on forward, it's amazing what was accomplished before the chip 
speed explosion and the world wide web.  I'm not knocking older, mature 
designs.  What started this whole spin-off thread was my assertion that 
storing dates and numbers with Display usage in files makes the design 
of the system more flexible for yet-unknown requirements.  Today, with 
large, cheap disks, I have the luxury of saying that.  In 1974, I 
probably would be singing a different tune.

>>One of the biggest lessons, in my opinion, is that systems are often 
>>asked to do things well outside of the narrow scope of their original 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> where a subcompact just won't do.

True as well.  I understand that concept.

> As my first programming instructor asked the class, Zen-like:
> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> or seen as being needed.

In every one of our network records, we have a "spare" field.  Part of 
that is to pad it out to an even word boundary (4 ASCII character, on 
the Unisys 2200), but part of it is for future use.  My supervisor is in 
charge of maintaining the requirements for every single field in the 
database (quite an impressive little folder he's accumulated over the 
years), and when a character or two from the spare field becomes pressed 
into service, he documents it.  Next major release, we name all the 
spare usage fields, change the (hopefully few) programs that use it, 
then reallocate spares, again, "for future use."

Our relational tables have changed that concept, just because it's so 
easy to add a column to one of those.  We don't use "Select *", so we 
won't impact existing programs.

    ELSE PERFORM CODE-LIKE-HELL UNTIL DAMNED-THING-WORKS.

signing checks for the system smile... then it runs.

…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>:)  It may (although our compiler doesn't support hex literals)...

Doesn't have to... define an 8-byte binary field, REDEFINE it as a PIC X.

0000 0000 = X'00', add 1 to get
0000 0001 = X'01', add 1 to get
0000 0010 = X'02', etc.

Since you are dealing with an ASCII numeric field for your year you'd lose 
everything between X'00' and X'29'... but start working with X'40' and you 
have something that could be made to work for the next 215 years or so...

... but don't let that give you any ideas.

[snip]

Curtis Bass wrote:

> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> is bloody dirt _CHEAP_ hardware.

This is also true.  :)

> In 1974, a thousand bytes of core
> (what we call "RAM" today) prabably cost one hundred thousand dollars.
>  Today, a GIGABYTE of RAM cost _LESS_ than _ONE_ thousand dollars. 
> When you "ponder" such things, sweating over two bytes per transaction
> seems myopic to disturbing extremes . . .

That's what some folks see as the end of some of the artistry that 
programming used to require.  I remember when I got a 100MB hard drive 
(and it wasn't cheap either!) - I thought, "Wow, I'll never fill this 
up!  I can install Windows and still have over 90MB free!"

Now I'm feeling crunched with 20GB.  I could alleviate that crunch if 
I'd get the courage to dump Windows - I just haven't found replacements 
for some of the apps I use from time to time.  Right now, I've been on 
straight Linux for a couple of weeks.

I do understand memory crunches, though.  Back when we were using the 
COBOL 74 compiler, we did a schema change that changed all instances of 
5-position codes in our database to 32-position codes.  There were maybe 
twenty or thirty of these occurrences.  We started having programs that 
wouldn't compile because working-storage was too big!  (Luckily, that 
gave us yet another reason to push for COBOL 85...)
```

photograph.

opposed to packed, fields in an outgoing file is _not_ the equivalent
of childless newlyweds buying an expensive mini van.  No, the
equivalent is the childless newlyweds getting that mini van for
_NEGLIGIBLE_ added cost, as insurance against whatever the future
brings.

of the trials, but, I suppose you could score a few pedant points if

No, Newton's Laws aren't suspect.  On the contrary, in your quest for
pedantry, you are simply ignoring the fact that we have an atmosphere
which can and does exert drag on all falling objects.  Heavier objects
are less influenced by this drag than lighter ones, all else being
equal, but the drag is still there no matter how heavy or light the
object, and even if its effect is too small to reliably observe.

In trying to devalue common sense, you unfortunately ended up
displaying a lack of same.  To be sure, that which we call "common
sense" can mislead, but to imply that it's universally useless is
unwarranted.  And common sense _can_ support an assertion, at least
until the assertion is proved wrong.

Maybe I'm not filled in entirely on this statement, but how is that an
incompatibility?  I would think it's just a barrier of consideration
for proper data exchange that seems to be getting more common between
PCs and the mainframe.  One must remember that PCs perform
little-endian processing by default, while the mainframes perform
big-endian processing.  

Not a compatibility issue but an interoperability one that can't be
"uncreated" by a programmer.

I dunno. Probably 80% of the Y2K problems were addressed in '98 & '99. We're
talking modifying 40 years worth of code, with modern tools and fewer
people. The job was done and Y2K turned out to be a non-event.

Consider a program written in, say, 1969 for a 64K IBM-360. By putting in

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2004-08-01T00:20:28+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<410c0940.103631892@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <9Dp3u7wuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407311239.23cdc2b0@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>format.

The crap is deleted records. If the C-ISAM file was just reorganized, it is an
ascii text file (assuming no comp/packed). The weakness of this approach is the
requirement for an external definition such as a copybook. It is difficult for
ISAM to produce a self-defining file because the file system knows only about
keys, and nothing about dependencies. However my demo could have done it,
because it had turned the copybook into a schema.

There are many tools that export tables or entire databases to XML, including
schema information and constraints. This produces self-defining files that can
be imported without manual intervention. People who exchange a lot of data have
a method 'Just Send Table' that combines Export with FTP. Granted, they are
using a suitable format, as you said, but the process is automated. 

Alternatively, one can write a do-it-yourself that reads the data dictionary and
constructs dynamic SQL to extract and format the data. That gives one more
control. For instance, different formats depending on recipient .. some want
csv, others want XML. I just wrote such a tool in Cobol.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> You're right, but raw data files ARE exchanged. Carol received 21 of them. 

It wouldn't have made much difference if they were EDIFAC or XML or
CSV, raw data doesn't _mean_ anything unless you know what it means.
 
> Databases put a stop to that. 
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Boss:  Send a copybook. Let them figure it out.

While you may have sent junk files out in that way, it certainly isn't
a common practice for the very good reason that data files contain
information that _you_don't_want_others_to_get_.

In the stock inventory files is the cost price.  Would you send cost
prices to your customers ?  Would you send your customer list to your
competitors ?

Any _sensible_ site would filter the data and only send what was
required in an extract.

In any case databases are proprietry format, but so are most Cobol
file systems. It happens that the default for MF Cobol on Unix is
C-ISAM, but this does not make it suitable as an interchange file
(even if you don't understand this yet), nor does it make this the
only format that is or has been used.

'sending a file plus a copybook' does _NOT_ make it usbale, even if it
doesn't contain data that is confidential.

Perhaps this is just another reason why everywhere you worked went
bankrupt.

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>CSV, raw data doesn't _mean_ anything unless you know what it means.

XML files can be self-defining. When imported to a database or spreadsheet,
columns will have names like 'Part_Number' and 'UPC'. 

If it came from a mainframe shop, the name will likely be self-qualifying and
missing vowels --  'TPrdMst_PrtNmbr' -- but still decipherable. 

>> Boss: Send the product file to X.
>> RW:   Ok, I'll work up an extract. What fields do they want?
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>competitors ?

Only the most rudimentary system puts Cost in the Product master. Typically cost
and selling prices are in another table keyed by date. A product does not have a
single cost; it has historic and future costs. 

Same for Quantity On Hand. When processing an order, you don't care what the
quantity is that instant, you want to know how many will be on the shelf when
the picker arrives 12 hours hence. Typical resolution for that table is one
hour.

Both are examples of Lists. They are not Arrays in the Product master, they are
separate tables .. when designed well, per Codd et al. 

>Any _sensible_ site would filter the data and only send what was
>required in an extract.

I'll let you tell managers who send raw files that they are not sensible.

>In any case databases are proprietry format, but so are most Cobol
>file systems. It happens that the default for MF Cobol on Unix is
>C-ISAM, but this does not make it suitable as an interchange file
>(even if you don't understand this yet), nor does it make this the
>only format that is or has been used.

Nearly all ISAMs come with a tool that turns the indexed file into a flat file
-- IDCAMS, Realcopy, etc. Carol's tapes were probably created with IDCAMS.
```

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)

- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <9Dp3u7wuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407311239.23cdc2b0@posting.google.com> <410c0940.103631892@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407312349.535b2cb8@posting.google.com> <410cf434.43224866@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408011654.712a869e@posting.google.com> <410e335f.124943999@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> XML files can be self-defining. When imported to a database or spreadsheet,
> columns will have names like 'Part_Number' and 'UPC'. 
> 
> If it came from a mainframe shop, the name will likely be self-qualifying and
> missing vowels --  'TPrdMst_PrtNmbr' -- but still decipherable. 

It may or may not be 'decipherable', it may or may require
assumptions, these may or may not be correctly assumed.  They may, or
may not, be in foreign languages with missing vowels.

It only means something if you know what it means.  Guesses don't
count.

> Only the most rudimentary system puts Cost in the Product master. Typically cost
> and selling prices are in another table keyed by date. A product does not have a
> single cost; it has historic and future costs. 

Cost by date is useless for stock valuation (though it may be useful
for other purposes) - unless every single unit has a date on it. 
Typically there will be an average cost used for the stock valuation
report and for sales profitability.

> Same for Quantity On Hand. When processing an order, you don't care what the
> quantity is that instant, you want to know how many will be on the shelf when
> the picker arrives 12 hours hence. Typical resolution for that table is one
> hour.

Your experience is obviously quite different, the business model is
quite different.  What you say may well be true of those sites where
you have worked, but it is _NOT_ true of other sites where the
business model is quite different. For example the resupply cycle may
be hours in your case, and months in mine.

In all my clients the 'Quantity on Hand' (actually the free stock)
when entering an order is essential.  They are mostly talking to the
customer at that timne and when they say they will supply today they
_will_ supply (or not if it isn't).
 
> Both are examples of Lists. They are not Arrays in the Product master, they are
> separate tables .. when designed well, per Codd et al. 

They may be 'examples of lists' or they may be single fields depending
entirely on the business model as chosen by the client.  Which they
are is not something for the programmer to choose.

But how the system operates is irrelevant to the point (and is merely
deflection on your part).  In many cases there will be _some_ data
items in files, or indeed whole records, which are not to be disclosed
to suppliers, customers or competitors.

> >Any _sensible_ site would filter the data and only send what was
> >required in an extract.
> 
> I'll let you tell managers who send raw files that they are not sensible.

I don't have to.  I wouldn't have such managers as clients. In any
case, I have been known to tell managers such things as they think
unsuitable.

Also sending out raw unusable data to customers and suppliers will
only piss them off and _they_ will tell the managers, or more usually
the sales managers, that pissing them off is counter productive.  Then
it becomes an internal issue from the top about who will tell the
manager.

If this didn't happen then perhaps it is another reason why your
companies went belly up.


Perhaps your advice should be inverted.  Your claimn was that
_no_file_ should have binary of BCD fields in it "because you might
use them as data interchange".  This is _very_ poor advice.  The data
files should be coded and obscured as much as possible so that no one
is tempted to send them to a customer (or competitor).
```

No, stock valuation is most often by FIFO or LIFO. Average cost is a distant

shipments are scheduled, which is almost always FIFO.

answer, but it's better suited to an accounting newsgroup than a programming
one.

>use them as data interchange".  This is _very_ poor advice.  The data
>files should be coded and obscured as much as possible so that no one
>is tempted to send them to a customer (or competitor).

Security that depends on obfuscation is amateurish and doomed to fail. The best
secutity schemes, such as DES, are public domain.

```
"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote:

>Robert Wagner wrote:
>> riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>No, mostly for manufacturing. In retail, nobody knows from FIFO or LIFO.

When preparing a purchase order, you need to know what the cost will be two
weeks hence. 

When checking a Direct Store Delivery (chips, soft drink) invoice, you need to
know what the cost was on the delivery date, a week ago. 

When processing a credit memo under the Retail Method Of Inventory Control,
where the store is billed both cost and retail, you need to know both as of the
delivery date. 

When setting retail prices, you need to know what the cost will be next week. If
you don't recognize cost changes until an invoice arrives, your retails will
always be two weeks behind. That's a lousy business model. When prices are going
up, you're losing money; when they're going down, you're not competitive. 

If retailers know nothing about LIFO and FIFO, explain why the WalMart annual
report says:

"We value our inventories at the lower of cost or market as determined primarily
by the retail method of accounting, using the last-in,
first-out (�LIFO�) method for substantially all U.S. merchandise inventories,
except SAM�S CLUB merchandise, which is based on average
cost using the LIFO method. Inventories for foreign operations are primarily
valued by the retail method of accounting and are stated
using the first-in, first-out (�FIFO�) method."

http://media.corporate-ir.net/media_files/irol/11/112761/reports/wmt_040704.pdf
(page 25)
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> When preparing a purchase order, you need to know what the cost will be two
> weeks hence. 

No. You need to know what price the supplier will charge you, which
they will tell my clients _now_, or indeed have already told them for
the current period.  Of course there are always products for which the
price will be completely unknown 'in two weeks time', such as oil or
pork bellies.  That is what futures contracts are for.  There are also
exchange rates to cater for, that is what forward exchange contracts
are for.

> When checking a Direct Store Delivery (chips, soft drink) invoice, you need to
> know what the cost was on the delivery date, a week ago. 

But you don't need to look it up in a separate table keyed by product
and date.  The delivery (which may be parts of several purchase
orders) is costed by including freight, customs charges (for imports)
and local carriage and the actual complete costs calculated and put
into the actual purchase order delivery file while the new average
cost of the stock is calculated.

You get the cost of a delivery item by looking up the delivery item.

How hard is that ?
 
> When processing a credit memo under the Retail Method Of Inventory Control,
> where the store is billed both cost and retail, you need to know both as of 
> the delivery date. 

In systems where there are order/invoices, a credits pick up the
actual cost and price (which may have been a special or quoted or
discounted) from the original order/invoice.  The number credited is
put back into the original order/invoice to ensure that duplicate
credits cannot be issued.

How hard is that ?

Where it is simply a POS system (which is not my clients) one could
record the cost on the computer record of the sale and then a credit
(which cannot be done without the sales slip) could track back to the
actual sales record.  Again this could ensure that credits were not
duplicated.

Of course if it were unit tracking using serial numbers then the
actual cost of the unit would be known.

> When setting retail prices, you need to know what the cost will be next week. If
> you don't recognize cost changes until an invoice arrives, your retails will
> always be two weeks behind. 

If you don't know how the actual buisiness works your assumptions will
be completely wrong.

> That's a lousy business model. 

And your wrongness will lead you to invalid and irrelavant
conclusions.

> When prices are going
> up, you're losing money; when they're going down, you're not competitive. 

So, what you are saying is that a business (every business) needs to
be able to tell its customers exactly what the price will be in two
weeks time while changing the price every day depending on what their
suppliers tell them.  Geez Robert, not only are you a marvel at
knowing how to run everyone's business but you are also a clairvoyant.

(BTW that was sarcasm).

> If retailers know nothing about LIFO and FIFO, explain why the WalMart annual
> report says:

Because not all businesses are WalMart.  Not only that but WalMart is
not all businesses.
 
_Some_ businesses use LIFO and/or FIFO.  _Some_ businesses use average
cost and don't care about LIFO/FIFO, _Some_ businesses use individual
unit tracking. _Some_ businesses use all of these. _Some_ businesses
use something else entirely (such as assessed value).

You may note that for some products WalMart uses avrerage cost.
```

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>the current period.  

At companies where I worked, if suppliers didn't tell us two weeks ahead, we
ignored the price on the invoice and paid the old price for two weeks. After the
first go around, most of them started telling us two weeks ahead. 

>Of course there are always products for which the
>price will be completely unknown 'in two weeks time', such as oil or
>pork bellies.  That is what futures contracts are for.  There are also
>exchange rates to cater for, that is what forward exchange contracts
>are for.

Correct. Transfer risk from producers and users, who aren't gamblers, to
speculators, who are. Risk then has a fixed cost, which can be built into the
product cost.

>> When checking a Direct Store Delivery (chips, soft drink) invoice, you need
to
>> know what the cost was on the delivery date, a week ago. 
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>cost of the stock is calculated.

an 'open PO' to restock the shelf every x days.

>> When processing a credit memo under the Retail Method Of Inventory Control,
>> where the store is billed both cost and retail, you need to know both as of 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>How hard is that ?

That's a good solution.

>> When setting retail prices, you need to know what the cost will be next week.
If
>> you don't recognize cost changes until an invoice arrives, your retails will
>> always be two weeks behind. 
>
>If you don't know how the actual buisiness works your assumptions will
>be completely wrong.

In the end, businesses buy stuff and resell it. How many ways can that 'work'?

If you want to get theoretical, the selling price should not be a function of
cost. It should be a function of supply and demand. But that makes the business
into a speculator betting on demand. In the long run, speculators lose, witness
Enron.


>> When prices are going
>> up, you're losing money; when they're going down, you're not competitive. 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>knowing how to run everyone's business but you are also a clairvoyant.

Thank you.  

>(BTW that was sarcasm).

In that case, cancel the thank you.

>> If retailers know nothing about LIFO and FIFO, explain why the WalMart annual
>> report says:
>
>Because not all businesses are WalMart.  Not only that but WalMart is
>not all businesses.

Yes they are. They all use GAAP

> _Some_ businesses
>use something else entirely (such as assessed value).

Not in the US. Gains cannot be recorded until an arms-length sale is made. 
Even readily valued assets such as stocks cannot be 'marked to market' except in
special circumstances. It is too easy to manipulate assessments. 

>You may note that for some products WalMart uses average cost.

"SAM'S CLUB merchandise, which is based on average cost using the LIFO method" 

That means.. 100 widgets on the shelf cost 1.00, 100 incoming widgets cost 1.20,
average cost is now 1.10. Assuming older widgets are shipped first and we're now
down to 100, how much did they cost? Average cost says 1.10; LIFO says 1.20;
FIFO says 1.00.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>> if suppliers didn't tell us two weeks ahead, we
>> ignored the price on the invoice and paid the old price for two weeks. After
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Would a mom-and-pop be able to do that ?

Of course not. Suppliers must like big companies because they give volume
discounts not offered to mom-and-pops. 

>That may well be true of some supermarkets here too, if any of these
>want to be my client then I may well do it the way that _they_ want.

Outside contractors are expected to do things the way the client wants, right or
wrong. Employees can (re)design systems to make them work right. 

>> In the end, businesses buy stuff and resell it. How many ways can that
'work'?
>
>No, wrong. _SOME_ businesses 'buy stuff and resell it'.  Other
>businesses make stuff, other businesses sell services. The number of
>ways it can work is potentially as many as the number of businesses
>there are.

Manufacturers and service businesses purchase labor, mark it up and sell it.
They book it as Cost of Goods Sold.
 
>Regardless, I am sure that _some_ companies, even in the US, will use
>assessed value for at least part of their valuation, even if only for
>WIP - or something else entirely.

WIP is usually expensed when incurred. In rare cases, it can be capitalized
(shown as an asset) but only to the extent of charges incurred. 

GAAP is often criticized for not reflecting the value of patents, R&D, exclusive
contracts, appreciated real estate, and the like. Investors argue the present
value of future cash flows is a company asset. Accountants think it is too easy
to manipulate.
```

"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:4110b936.290303871@news.optonline.net...
> riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
> Of course not. Suppliers must like big companies because they give volume
> discounts not offered to mom-and-pops.

Well, no, that's not quite accurate. (In the USA, for firms engaged in
Interstate Commerce)

The suppliers offer the same volume discounts to mom and pop  - as they
must, pursuant to the 1963 Robinson-Patman Act, an amendment to the 1901
Sherman Ant-Trust Act. (Meaning any damages awarded in an action brought
under the Act are automatically trebled). (An exception is granted in the
presence of specific competitive information).

It's just that mom and pop can't afford to take advantage of those volume
discounts.

MCM

- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <217e491a.0407312349.535b2cb8@posting.google.com> <410cf434.43224866@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408011654.712a869e@posting.google.com> <410e335f.124943999@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408021131.61012e63@posting.google.com> <410ec7fb.162994088@news.optonline.net> <MqqdnVFhtKUJcpPcRVn-tw@giganews.com> <410f31d0.190090825@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408031257.915e9ba@posting.google.com> <41101687.248651143@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0408032256.2376df72@posting.google.com> <4110b936.290303871@news.optonline.net> <iV4Qc.147$r_1.78808@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com>`

```
"Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:4110b936.290303871@news.optonline.net...
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>discounts.

Very good!

By coincidence, I just finished a one-year project working on a F-100 company's
Rebates and Discounts system. Rather than discussing it, I'll talk about a
friend who audits invoices for supermarkets. He looks for 'overlooked' discounts
and files claims with the vendors, keeping half of what he recovers. On an
average-sized ($2B/yr) client that's well run, he recovers $1M. If it's not well
run, $4-5M. 

He does it with a database containing discounts given to other clients. On each
audit, he's doing two things: looking for missed discounts and adding discounts
that _were_ given to his database. 

He works in an industry that is well aware of RPA. When I've mentioned it in
other industries, I got blank looks in return. There are major industries that
routinely ignore RPA. 

Some think the penalty is three times the discount. As you point out, it is
three times "damages" i.e. lost business, which is usually many times the
discount denied. 

"Specific competitive information" made an exception for customers in different
geographic markets. It is about to be overturned for internet merchants, to whom
geography doesn't matter. They include WalMart, Sears, Office Depot, etc.

On a related note (trying to get back on topic), the Sarbanes-Oxley Act imposes
severe criminal penalties, including up to a 20-year prison term, for (a)
destroying, altering, or falsifying records with the intent to impede an
investigation "of any matter within the jurisdiction of any department or agency
of the United States," (b) tampering with a record "with intent to impair the
object's integrity or availability for use in an official proceeding,".  Well,
antitrust is a Federal matter. A programmer who destroys (deletes) or falsifies
rebate information could be looking at an involuntary career change.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> No, stock valuation is most often by FIFO or LIFO. Average cost is a distant
> third. It is not necessary to identify individual units, only to know how
> shipments are scheduled, which is almost always FIFO.

Once again you claim 'what you have seen' as being 'most' if not all
of what happens.  While what you say may well be true for some of the
types of businesses that you have worked at, it is by no means the
only, or even the most common.

In my clients the most common is 'average cost'.  This is their
requirement, this is how their business operates.  Some clients have
no interest in FIFO or LIFO, all stock is just stock, undated,
non-expiring.  Others are batch orientated where they ship by
_specific_batch_.  Not FIFO or LIFO, but by customer requirements for
specified batch number.  Yet others track by serial number.

Different businesses have different needs, something your ideas don't
appear to consider.
 
> If so, why doesn't the accounting model follow FIFO reality? 

It isn't so. Many don't care about FIFO, except in the most general
way, it's irrelevant.

> >Your experience is obviously quite different, the business model is
> >quite different.  What you say may well be true of those sites where
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> >_will_ supply (or not if it isn't).
 
> Which is it? The first paragraph says months, the second says same day.

Geez, Robert, the two paragraphs are _different_ things.  The first
specifies that the resupply cycle may be months (and they get several
months supply at a time).  The second paragraph talks about selling to
a customer.  You do understand the difference between stock purchasing
and selling don't you ?

> I'll wager both got the same solution -- a single Quantity on Hand. 

In some systems a single QOH is all that is required, in others the
stock is accounted for in different branch/locations, yet others are
batch or pallet based and so have many quantities to make up the total
QoH.

Such variety is required to cater efficiently for the different
business models.

> >They may be 'examples of lists' or they may be single fields depending
> >entirely on the business model as chosen by the client.  Which they
> >are is not something for the programmer to choose.
> 
> Systems Designers often collaborate in forming the business model.

NO, WRONG. The Business Model should never be subordinate to the
computer system design.  A Systems _Analyst_ may well be used to
determine what the Business Model _IS_ in order that the computer can
be used as a tool to automate it.
 
> >Perhaps your advice should be inverted.  Your claim was that
> >_no_file_ should have binary of BCD fields in it "because you might
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> secutity schemes, such as DES, are public domain.

You completely miss the point, yet again, do try and keep up.  The
obscuring has _nothing_ to do with security, it has everything to do
with ensuring that managers are not tempted to 'send the file'
anywhere, well not tempted a second time.
```

riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>only, or even the most common.

I've worked with financial statements (10-Q) of ALL publically traded US
companies. I'm very familiar with GAAP (accounting principles). This is how
accounting works, whether you realize it or not.

>In my clients the most common is 'average cost'.  This is their
>requirement, this is how their business operates.  Some clients have
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>specified batch number.  Yet others track by serial number.

That's what they tell you, or what you see operationally. Average cost is
adequate when inventory moves slowly or there's little competition or margins
are sky high. If you mention it to an American supermarket company or general
merchandiser, they'll laugh at you.

>Different businesses have different needs, something your ideas don't
>appear to consider.

Not really. Generally Accepted Accounting Principles (GAAP) apply to all
industries.

>> >Your experience is obviously quite different, the business model is
>> >quite different.  What you say may well be true of those sites where
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>and selling don't you ?

Efficient industries don't store several months supply in a warehouse, they
'pick from the receiving dock'. My software ran a produce warehouse that had a
.9 day supply, including non-perishibles such as potatoes. We printed on the
purchase order 'ship to arrive at dock xx at 1:00 pm' and assumed it would be
there. The shipping program picked it from there without it being 'put up' to a
storage bin. 

In the long run, there is no difference between purchasing and selling. A
warehouse (and retail store) is a cache, a place to hold merchandise
temporarily. The shorter the hold time, the more efficient the business.
Conversely, the longer the hold time, the sloppier the business. American
supermarket warehouses and stores hold, on average, a two week supply at each. 

>> >They may be 'examples of lists' or they may be single fields depending
>> >entirely on the business model as chosen by the client.  Which they
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>be used as a tool to automate it.

You see systems analysts as computer techies, ignorant of business, whose role
is translating business requirements into technical implementations. I see
systems analysts understanding both business and computing .. being able to
translate GAAP into database design, for example. 

If you were a CEO, which would you want on the design team?

riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

> I see Systems Analysts as appreciating a _wide_ range of business
>models and willing to _learn_ about more different types and different
>needs, rather than attempting to impose preconceived ideas about what
>the business should be doing.

When I was a consultant visiting many companies, I noticed the first thing out
of the client's mouth was usually 'This business is unique; it's unlike any
other you've seen.' Other consultants noticed it as well. It became an inside
joke. After the client said it, we'd look at each other and make an effort to
suppress laughter. 

Why do managers feel the need to be in a 'unique' business? 

I had a friend who sold software to large restaurant chains. After looking at
it, I said "This is a standard manufacturing system. All you did was change
terminology. Formula is now Recipe, Raw Material is now Ingredient, measurements
are in cups rather than pounds, Standard Cost is called Portion Cost." He
answered, "You and I know that, but THEY don't. They think I wrote it expressly
for the Food Service industry. I say so in marketing material. It's what they
want to hear."

When I was in the distribution industry, we actually maintained all three
costs above plus 'last cost' and 'replacement cost.'

I know we used average cost when we did general "stock valuation" and
"replacement cost" to calculate cost-of-goods for purposes of sales
commission (we paid commission on gross margin); and our accountant told me
he used LIFO cost for tax purposes.

Since I am not an accountant, I don't understand the tax thing; but as an
'operations' guy the average/replacement costs used as above make perfect
sense to me.

It would seem maintaining multiple costs offers the best of all worlds.

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net...
> >   I think e.g. of the floating point format as specified by IEEE; on
> >the other hand, I think that machine-specific formats as the 'little-
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> They have no place in ANY files.

How about in systems where other software is in use; software which cannot
'natively' read/write COBOL-format numerics?  e.g., Intel-based systems
using programs written in "C" or BASIC or FORTRAN to which COBOL USAGE
BINARY or PACKED-DECIMAL data are alien?

Sure, you could define all numeric data with USAGE DISPLAY, which can be
used by any program in the system, but that could create a terrible waste of
storage space, and require all software (including the COBOL-based software)
to effect a character-string-to-true-numeric conversion whenever arithmetic
is required.

Better I would think that such systems use centrally controlled copy
libraries, one for each such shared file for each language product in use.
(Not that centrally-controlled copy libraries shouldn't be in use anyway for
files acccesed by many programs within a system).


MCM

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2004-08-01T18:37:47+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
"Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net...
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>BINARY or PACKED-DECIMAL data are alien?

All three languages can natively handle type 'int', which is Cobol's BINARY.

BINARY should be avoided because it gums up data transmission and EBCDIC-ASCII
conversion. 

>Sure, you could define all numeric data with USAGE DISPLAY, which can be
>used by any program in the system, but that could create a terrible waste of
>storage space, and require all software (including the COBOL-based software)
>to effect a character-string-to-true-numeric conversion whenever arithmetic
>is required.

Disk space and bandwidth are cheap. Computers are fast, and no longer live in a
glass room 'island'. Being able to communicate with other computers is more
important than saving a few milliseconds. You are applying yesteryear's
solutions to today's problems.

>Better I would think that such systems use centrally controlled copy
>libraries, one for each such shared file for each language product in use.
>(Not that centrally-controlled copy libraries shouldn't be in use anyway for
>files acccesed by many programs within a system).

We have that. After wrenching control of data from applications programmers, we
put it in a database, where the centrally controlled description is called a
schema. Every language can read the data. So can non-language tools such as
spreadsheets, report generators and data warehousing products. Best of all,
they don't have to be running on the same machine. A program running on a
desktop can directly access databases all over the world .. without caring what
brand they are. 

Want a record layout? Just type 'DESCribe tablename'.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-08-02T09:58:17-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<celrr9$30th$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <9Dp3u7wuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net> <Oe4Pc.3617$TE6.2302@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <410d2cba.57697060@news.optonline.net> <YYqPc.2095$hI.630556@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com> <celme1$l90$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <mCtPc.2238$hI.654797@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com>`

```
"Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote in message
news:mCtPc.2238$hI.654797@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com...
> The COBOL orientation is true on all platforms.

That may *happen to be* the case; I can't address that.

What I can say is that the COBOL standard makes no such requirement -- "The
USAGE BINARY clause specifies that a radix of 2 is used to represent a
numeric item in the storage of the computer.  Each implementor specifies the
precise effect of the USAGE BINARY clause upon the alignment and
representation of the data item in the storage of the computer, including
the representation of any algebraic sign.  Sufficient computer storage shall
be allocated by the implementor to contain the maximum range of values
imposed by the associated decimal picture character-string."

As far as the standard is concerned, the "computer storage" containing a
USAGE BINARY item can be a circle of marble columns with the value
represented by whether the column is standing or lying down (or by whether
the column has a Doric or an Ionic capital).  The standard does not care at
which point in the circle the determination of value starts, or in which
direction it proceeds, or how many columns there are (so long as there are
enough!).  Those are implementation details.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Layout Hell-o

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2004-08-04T16:28:03+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cer2qj09p0@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<cec34l$2h5t$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <9Dp3u7wuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <410ad0b4.23607858@news.optonline.net> <Oe4Pc.3617$TE6.2302@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <410d2cba.57697060@news.optonline.net> <YYqPc.2095$hI.630556@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com> <celme1$l90$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <mCtPc.2238$hI.654797@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com>`

```

In article <mCtPc.2238$hI.654797@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com>, "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> writes:
> >
> > On  2-Aug-2004, "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Intel order

The C standard requires that integral types use a "pure binary
numeration system" representation (ISO 9899-1990 6.1.2.5), with
various other restrictions, and in effect require one of sign-
magnitude, one's-complement, or two's-complement.  I don't know of a
conforming implementation which uses anything other than two's-
complement.  However, I don't believe there's any requirement that
the representation match the "native" pure binary representation used
by the platform - if there is one.

In practice, C implementations essentially always do use the native
integer representation, however, which means that they have big-endian
integers if the CPU uses big-endian integers.  For Windows running on
a big-endian CPU (which was an option, once upon a time), C would use
big-endian integers.  For Unix, C implementations are either big- or
little-endian depending on the CPU, usually; though for bimodal CPUs
the implementor has to choose the appropriate representation based on
the mode in which they expect the program to run.

There is occasional talk of adding endian-specific type support to C,
but insufficient interest exists to push it through the standard
committee, probably because C's bitwise operators make it trivial to
do the conversion, and utility macros and functions to do it are widely
available anyway.  (And adding it to the C standard now would be an
exercise in futility, since few implementations have adopted the 1999
standard.)

As for BASIC - well, AFAIK, the dominant implementation of a language
calling itself "BASIC" doesn't follow any external standard anyway.
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:celln3$kqp$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 30-Jul-2004, robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
>
> > In the last 30 years, I haven't described a file with COMP-? types. COMP
is
> > for internal efficiency; files should be 100% DISPLAY.
>
> Often Packed was as efficient as COMP.
>

In some implementations, COMP *was and still is* the same as packed-decimal
(capitalized or not).

All that the COBOL standard requires of USAGE COMP is that it be able to
represent a numeric item; the radix and format are defined by the
implementor.  USAGE PACKED-DECIMAL (new in '85) requires that a radix of 10
be used in the representation and that each digit position occupy the
minimum possible configuration on computer storage; otherwise the format
(including sign representation) is defined by the implementor.

    -Chuck Stevens
```
